Car il a mout de sanc dedens le cors qui est ja eschaufez, et fait venir fievre; si en covient traire grant quantiteit, et eslargir le pertus tant qu’il chiece em pameson; puis que tu rewardes toutes les conditions de sainie, et met ta main sus som poulz quant il saine, que li mors ne viegne en leu de pameson, et ceu avient maintes fois, quant li sainieres est negligens; et ne covient mie quant tu vues desliier lou bras, et faire issir sanc la seconde fois, et li pertus est clos, que tu presses forment sus; ne s’il est corbes, qu’il estende lou bras hastanment, car ce seroit malz; mais le laisse tant qu’il saine la seconde fois; ou tu i point a la pointe dou flaime, tant que li sans qui est congelez a pertus de la voinne isse fuer, et met sus I poc de seil remis en aigue, et presse sus legierement.	Because he has much blood inside the body which is already heated up and brings on fever; so it needs to be drawn out in great quantity, and the opening should be widened as much as possible until it flows into a basin; then you must reward all the conditions of sainie, and put your hand over some hairs when he bleeds, that death may not come in place of the basin, and such things happen many times when the surgeon is neglectful; and it does not need to be done when you see untie the arm and make blood flow a second time, and the opening is closed, that you press strongly on it; nor if it is curved should you stretch out the arm hastily, for this would be bad; but leave it so until he bleeds a second time; or you point with the tip of the flame as long as the frozen blood which has been congealed flows out through the opening of the vein, and put on a piece of cloth dipped in water, and press lightly on it.
Et se li voine est enflee, si la laisse et ne l’estreindre mie, tant que li aposteme soit apaisiez.	And if the abscess is swollen, leave it alone and do not constrict it, until the swelling has subsided.
Et se necessitez est dou traire dou sanc la seconde fois, si le saine en celui leu meisme, ou en un autre voinne, ou en l’autre bras.	And if you need to let out blood the second time, then he heals in that same place, or in another abscess, or in the other arm.
Li voinne emmi le bras est sainie en leu de la voine dou chief por ceu qu’elle est composte.	The wound on the arm is healed in place of the swelling of the head because it was composed.
Et li maniere de la sainie si est que li malaides boute sa main en aigue chaude, tant que li foscile soit rouges; puis soit liiéz moienement, si que li voinne apere bien, puis le saine maintenant dou travers, ne mie dou large ne dou lonc; et soit li pertus larges, et soit sainiez bien pres de la jointe de la main, et se sans n’en puet issir, si remet la main en aigue chaude, et laisse corre lou sanc en l’aigue, tant que tu en aies assez; et se c’est en esteit, si ne rebouter mie la main en aigue chaude, car on l’i met plus en yver que en esteit; et li sainie de ceste voine est plus sauve de toutes autres voinnes, qu’il n’i a ne artere ne nerf.	And the manner of healing is such that the patient puts his hand into hot water, so that the scab will be red; then he binds it moderately, so that the vein appears well, then he cuts now through the middle, not too long or wide; and the incision should be large, and the healing should be done very close to the joint of the hand, and if pus cannot come out, then put the hand back into hot water, and let the blood stay in the water until you have enough; and if it is cold, do not put the hand again into hot water, for it is colder than at home; and the healing of this vein is more safe from all other veins, there are no arteries or nerves.
Li sainie de la salvatele de la main destre si vaut a toutes les maladies dou foie, et c’est li maniere: estrain la main, et boute en aigue chaude, tant que li voinne soit enflee, si c’on la voie; et se la saine de travers, et se tu la copes dou tout en tout, si ne grieve rien; et n’aler mie trop em parfont, car li nerf sont desoz toz desnuez de char, et puis remet la main en aigue chaude, se li sans est congelez en la plaie, et n’en puet issir.	The healing of the wound on the right hand is worth for all the diseases of the liver, and this is the manner: strain the hand and put it into hot water until the vein appears; and if the incision is crooked, and you cut through everything in one go, it will not hurt anything; and do not go too deep, because the nerves are under a layer of naked flesh, and then put the hand back into hot water if the pus is frozen in the wound and cannot come out.
Et quant tu avras trait dou sanc tant com mestier t’est, si met sus la voinne oile et seil por souder hastanment, et ensi fai de chescun rain.	And when you have treated the wound with oil as long as it is needed, then put a compress on the abscess to close it quickly, and do this for each pain. (Note: "hastanment" means "quickly", and "chescun rain" is an archaic way of saying "each time")
Li sainie de li en la main senestre si vaut a la maladie de la mice, et fai tout ausi com je t’ai dit devers destre.	The cure for the abscess in the left hand is worth for the disease of the spleen, and do everything similarly as I told you about the right one.
Li sainie de la sophene si vaut a toutes les maladies que sont desoz le cors, si com maladies de la marris, et retenement de flors, et maladie des rains, et des cuixes et des jambes ancienes, et semblans maladies.	The blood of the spleen is valuable for all diseases that are under the body, such as illnesses of the liver, and retention of flowers, and disease of the reins, and of the hips and old legs, and similar diseases.
Et li qualitez de sa sainie si est que li malaides boute som piet en aigue chaude, et frote sus la voinne tant qu’elle pere, et lie desus la jointure dou piet; et li voinne si est a la cheville, et par desus lou piet en vont mout de rains; saine la donques ou plus large rain, ou a la cheville, que mues vaut.	And the quality of its blood is that the patient throws his foot into hot water and rubs on the vein until it bleeds, and ties above the joint of the foot; and the vein is at the ankle, and above the foot there goes much blood; bleed therefore the largest vein, or at the ankle, that is worth more.
Et se tu la saines sus lou piet, si te warde des ners qui sont desoz, et la saine de traver ausi com en copant; et soit li flaimes anesil.	And if you cure it above the foot, then beware of the nerves that are below, and the cure goes through as well when cutting; and the scar will be thin.
Et se li sans n’en puet issir, si remet lou piet en l’aigue, et laisse bien vuidier la voine.	And if the pus cannot come out, then put the foot in water again, and let it drain well from the abscess.
Et se li sainieres faut la premiere fois, si rencomence la seconde au desus un poc, car li leus est salz; ne ne douter de nerf, si com nos avons dit, et ensi fai de la sophene de l’autre piet igalment.	And if the bloodletting fails the first time, it should be repeated again on top of a cloth, because the place is salty; do not doubt the nerves, as we have said, and so make an incision of the spleen from the other foot in the same way.
Li leus de la voinne sciatique est a la cheville de fuer, et sa sainie vaut a la dolor de la hainche, quant c’est por sanc chaut.	The sore of the sciatic vein is at the ankle of the back leg, and its cure is worth for the pain in the hip, when it's due to hot blood.
Et li qualitez de sa sainie si est que li malaides entre ou bain, et estraigne desus la cheville IIII dois en tordant, car autrement n’apert li voinne; et s’elle apert, si le saine en toutes les manieres que tu pues, et en traversant; et vaut mues que tu la copes, car li leus est saus, et elle est mout reponue a mains homes.	The qualities of his healthiness are such that the patient enters into the bath, and strains over the ankle four times in twisting, for otherwise the vein will not open; and if it opens, then he lets it bleed in all ways that you can, and crosswise; and what is worth noting is that you should cover him, because the place is sound, and it has been reopened by many people.
Et se tu ne la trueves, si saine aucun rain de li qui apert ou dos dou piet, et vers lou petit doi, et te warde des ners; et fai tant corre dou sanc que tu vues, et puis deslie lou lien, et met sus coton et l’estraint; ele est maintenant sanee.	And if you do not find it, then bleed some vein of his that opens at the back of the foot, and towards the small toe, and beware of the nerves; and let out as much blood as you see, and then release the ligature, and put on cotton and bind it; now he is healed.
des sainies de ventouse	bloodlettings with a bellows (or possibly "with suction")
Ventouses sont fait de cornes, ou d’arain, ou de voirre; et ventosation si est faite selonc .ij. manieres: ou par ferir et atraire sanc, ou sens ferir.	Ventouses are made of horns, or copper, or glass; and ventousation is done in two ways: either by making an incision to draw out blood, or without making an incision.
Et ceste ventosation qui est sens ferir si est selonc II manieres: ou elle est a tout feu, ou sens feu; et ventosation qui est faite par ferir et par estraction de sanc si est faite en XIIII leus dou cors, de coi li uns est tout en som le chief; et li autres est emmi le col; et II autres en II costeis dou col; et li ventouse desoz lou menton; et li ventouse des II espaules; et li ventouse sus les crupies; et les ventouses emmi les II bras; et les ventouses des II jambes; et les ventouses des II chevilles; et li ventosation ne trait point de sanc, fuer que des menues voinnes que sont esparses en la char, et por ceu n’en afoiblist om mie, si com on fait de sainie de voinne; et ne covient mie qu’elle soit aministree, ne a tout feu ne sens feu, en aucune maladie qui vient de repletion, tant que toz li cors soit vuidiez.	And this bellows treatment which is without hurting oneself is in two manners: either it is all hot, or not hot; and the bellowing that is made by cutting and extracting blood is done in fourteen places of the body, because one is entirely on the top of the head; and another is under the neck; and two others are between the shoulders; and the bellows below the chin; and the bellows on both shoulders; and the bellows above the buttocks; and the bellows in both arms; and the bellows in both legs; and the bellows in both ankles; and the bellowing does not treat blood, except for the small veins that are scattered in the flesh, and because of this it has not weakened anyone, as is done with vein bleeding; and it should not be administered, nor all hot nor not hot, in any disease that comes from repletion, until the entire body is emptied.
Et se par necessiteit de maladies covient ventoser, ou por acostumances, si l’amenistrons chescune houre en l’encomencement dou mois, ou en la fin, et emmi et en chescun tens; car il i a de telz qui, quant il ont mestier de ventoser, si ont pesant chief; et si a telz qui truevent repletion et roujor en lor faice, et en lor chief, et au col, et tenebrour as eulz; et telz qui truevent une goute de sanc en lor bouche, et ont enflee la lengue, et raichent sanc; et si i a de telz qui songent, et voient par nut sanc et navrez et mors et semblans choses.	And if by necessity of illnesses it is necessary to bleed, or for habituation, we should have each hour in the beginning of the month, or at the end, and within and in each time; for there are such people who, when they need to be bled, so have a heavy head; and there are those who find repletion and redness in their face, and in their head, and in their neck, and cloudiness over their eyes; and there are those who find a drop of blood in their mouth, and have swollen tongue, and hemorrhage; and there are those who sleep, and see through bloody fluid and wounded and dead and similar things.
Et quant nos voions ces signes, et meismement quant c’est emmi lou mois, nos lou commandons a ventoser aprés .iij. houres dou jor.	And when we see these signs, and just the same when it's in the month, we order them to be ventoused after three hours of the day.
Li sainie de la muze si aide a la grieteit dou chief … que vient as eulz, et ne covient mie que ce soit aprés l’evacuation de tout lou cors; et ceste ventosation si est en leu de la sainie de la cephalique, et ne covient mie que cil qui est de froide complexion en use, ou qui ait chatharre, car ce lor fait mout grant nuisement; et por ceu ne covient mie que li veillart en usent, ne qui ont ou chief froides maladies.	The bloodletting of the nose so helps the inflammation of the head that comes to the eyes, and it is not necessary that this be after the evacuation of all the body; and this bleeding is in place of the cephalic bloodletting, and it is not fitting for those who have a cold complexion to use it, or who have catarrh, because this does them great harm; and therefore it is not fitting for old people to use it, nor those with cold head illnesses.
Et cil qui s’en saine sovent, si devient oblious, et por ce covient mettre la ventouse un poc plus aval par la doutance d’obliance.	And those who cure themselves with it often become forgetful, and for this reason the ventouse should be placed a bit lower by the back of the head, in order to counteract forgetfulness.
Li ventosation emmi le col est en leu de la sainie dou chief et de la basilique, et por ceu vaut elle a l’estrecement dou pis, et a fendure de la chenal dou pormon, et a toust, et a repletion; et la covient poc bouter, car s’elle tient trop fermement, si enjanre obliance ou stomac et ou cuer.	The sweating sickness on the neck is in place of the wound of the head and of the vertebrae, and for this reason it is worth it to the constriction of the back, and the rupture of the channel of the womb, and at throat, and at repletion; and one must often put poultice, because if she holds too tightly, so she will make obliqueness or stomach and heart trouble.
Li ventosations as II costelz dou col si vaut as dolors dou chief, et obtalmie et emigrainne, et a l’estranglement, et a la dolor as racines des dens, et est en leu de la sainie de la basilique; et covient que tu comandes au sainor qu’il espresse sa main au mettre, qu’il ne cope l’artere, et ne veigne decorremens de sanc.	The sweatings around the two costal bones of the neck are worth as pains of the head, and eye pain and migraine, and at strangulation, and in the pain of the roots of the teeth, and it is in place of the wound of the vertebrae; and you must command the surgeon to express his hand in order to put, that he does not compress the artery, and does not take away excessively blood.
Li ventosations desoz lou menton si vaut a maladie de bouche, et a corruption de gencives, et semblans maladies qui sont en la bouche; et est en leu des sainies des .ij. levres.	The ventousation under the chin is worth for diseases of the mouth, and corruption of the gums, and similar illnesses that are in the mouth; and it's in place of the cures for the two lips.
Li ventosations des espaules vaut a maladie que vient de chalor et de remplissement.	The ventousation on the shoulders is worth for diseases that come from heat and fullness.
Li ventosations des coutes si vaut autant com des III voinnes dou bras, car elle atrait sanc de ces grosses voinnes; et comande au sainor qu’il ne fiere mie trop em parfont, car li leus est nus de char, et sait desoz ners et arteres.	The sweatings of the sides are worth as much as three veins of the arm, for they carry blood from these large veins; and command the surgeon that he does not make incision too deep, for the place is bare of flesh, and there are nerves and arteries underneath.
Li ventouse qui est sus la croupe si vaut as emoroydes dou fondement, et as maladies desoz, et covient que li ventouse soit grans, et soit d’arain, qu’elle a mestier de bien traire; et par aventure, s’elle estoit subtilz, si briseroit; et i fai grans plaies.	The ventouse on the backside is worth for hemorrhoids of the fundament and diseases below, and it's necessary that the ventouse be large and made of copper, so that it can draw well; and perhaps if it were subtle, it would break and cause great wounds.
Les ventouses des jambes si sainent apertement, car elles atraient sanc de tout le cors, et valent as dolors ancienes des rains, et de la marris, et de la vessie, et ramoinnent les flors; et sont en leu des .ij. sophenes, et des .ij. chevilles, et toutevoie elles afoiblissent lou cors aprés lonc us, et font venir paumison em plusors dispositions.	The ventouses on the legs are clear in their healing, because they draw blood from the whole body, and are worth for old pains of the reins, and the kidneys, and the bladder, and they clean out the stones; and they replace the two saphene veins, and the two ankle ventouses, but despite this, after long use they weaken the body and bring on a condition of wasting in many cases.
Li maniere de mettre la ventouse si est que tu la mettes premier vuide, et succe tempreement, et la met hastanment, et remue igalment, et ne cesser de ceu faire, tant que tu voies lou leu rouge et enfleit, et apere li roujor dou sanc; puis fier, et remet les ventouses, et pren warde en la disposition des cors, car il i a telz qui ont tanre char, et clers porez: si covient ferir une fois soulement, et covient faire larges plaies, et un poc parfondes; et suce legierement.	The way to apply the ventouse is that you first put it on empty, and then fill it with air in a temperate manner, and quickly place it on, and move it evenly, and do not stop doing this until you see the area red and swollen, and observe the redness of the blood; then cut, and reapply the ventouses, and be aware of the condition of the body, because there are such people who have thick flesh and clear pores: so it is necessary to cut only once, and make large and somewhat deep wounds, and suck lightly.
Et se li sans est gros, si couvient ferir .ij. fois: la premiere fois por subtiliier la voie dou sanc gros.	And if the blood is thick, it's necessary to cut twice: the first time to thin out the way of the thick blood.
Et se li sans est tres espés, si covient ferir la tierce fois; et quant nos volons traire dou sanc poc et poc, si nos soffist soulement une fois.	And if the blood is very thick, it's necessary to cut a third time; and when we want to draw out the blood little by little, it suffices with one single cut.
Et se nos volons mout de sanc, si rencomensons sovent; et se li sans est espés, si fier em parfont, et li termines si est que li keurs soit perciez soudement, et couvient a celui qui a gros cuir et dur, et qui ont estrois porelz, que tu oignes les leus des ventouses d’oile aperitif et dissolutif: se c’est en esteit, d’oile violet, ou d’oile d’amandres douces, ou d’oile de semence de cucurbite; et se c’est en yveir, d’oile de cresson, ou de lis, ou de camomille, ou autre semblant.	And if we want a lot of blood, then let us be careful often; and if the blood is thick, then we pierce it deep, and the extremities so that the quicks are pierced suddenly, and it is necessary for those who have hard skin and tough, and who have large pores, that you anoint the places of the poultices with oil which is aperitive and dissolvent: if this is in summer, with violet oil, or sweet almond oil, or cucumber seed oil; and if this is in autumn, with dandelion oil, or lily oil, or chamomile oil, or similar.
Et se li superfluitez est grosse et froide, si pren oile amaratin, ou de balsamite, ou oile d’anet, et les semblans.	And if the superfluities are thick and cold, then take bitter almond oil, or balm oil, or aniseed oil, and similar ones.
Et se cil cui on saine a tendre char et larges porelz, se n’i covient mettre point d’oile, mais le covient laver aprés la ventosation d’aigue rose, ou d’aigue froide, ou d’aigue de morelle, ou d’aigue de cucurbite, ou d’aigue de porcelainne, ou autre semblant.	And if the person to whom we give treatment has soft skin and large pores, then there is no need to put a point of oil, but it should be washed after the poulticing with rose water, or cold water, or vervain water, or cucumber seed water, or porcelain water, or similar.
Et cil qui a mout de moistor, si se leve d’aisil, ou d’aigue de mirte, et de sumac, et de semblans choses.	And those who have a lot of moisture, should rise from ashes, or wormwood water, and sumac, and similar things. (Note: "mout de moistor" likely means someone with a lot of bodily fluids or moisture)
Et cil qui ont grosses superfluitez se levent de vin viel, ou d’aigue de seuc, ou de decoction d’anet ou de camomile, ou d’autres semblans; et ne se covient mie sainier de ventouse devant le bain ne em bain, mais aprés lou bain une houre ou II; et ne dorme mie aprés la ventosacion, et covient k’il se warse, quant il se doit ventoser et quant il est ventosez.	And those who have thick superfluities are lifted up with old wine, or vinegar of rue, or decoction of wormwood or chamomile, or similar things; and they do not think to heal themselves by cupping before a bath nor in a bath, but after the bath an hour or two; and they must not sleep after the cupping, and it is necessary that he be warn when he should be cupped and when he is cupped.
Et warde se cil qui se saine est coleriques, et se li sans est trop agus et enflamez; si covient qu’il preigne choses froides, si com pomes grenates et endives et laitues a l’aisil, et semblans choses; et menjuce pucins, et char de chastron, et viandes moistes.	And warn those who are sick of the choleric temperament, and whose blood is too sharp and inflamed; it is necessary that he take cold things, such as green apples and endives and lettuce with vinegar, and similar things; and pomegranate juice, and mutton fat, and moist foods.
Et cil qui a froide complexion, si preigne syrop de miel, et vin odorant moien, qui est entre viel et noveil, et menjuce poc, et menjuce pucins et petis oiselz et pijons; et lou jor qu’il se ventousera, si doit plus boivre que mengier; et covient doner a aucuns homes aucune fois triaicle ou muscate devant la ventosacion, ou aprés, por conforter les membres principalz; et soit subtiliiez li sans, mais il ne covient mie que cil qui sont chaut le boivent.	And those who have a cold complexion, should take honey syrup and half-ripe wine which is between old and new, and pomegranate juice, and pomegranate juice and small birds and young chickens; and the day that he will be cupped, he must drink more than eat; and it is necessary to give some men threefold or muscadine before or after cupping for the comfort of the principal limbs; and the blood should be thinned, but those who are hot should not drink it.
Les ventouses que sont mises sens ferir sont celles que sont mises sus lou foie, et sus la mice, et podagre, et sus lou ventre et la boude et les rains et la hainche, car cil membre ne soffrent mie ferir sus oulz, et ne le fait on fuer que por atraire sanc de membre em membre, si com nos mettons sus les mameles quant li neis sainne; por oster la viscositeit qui est au membre, si com nos metons ventouses sus le ventre et sus la boude por amenuisier le membre; et oste la dolor, quant il i a opilation ou piere, si oste par sa vertu atractive par aventure l’opilation, ou desaert la piere de son leu; et ensi fait ele quant on le met sus lou foie et sus la mice quant il i a ventositeit retenue, et ces ventouses sont aministrees vuides por sucier tant soulement; et aucune fois sont aministrees ensamble feu, et aucunes fois plainnes d’aigue tede, si com em maladies dou pleuresin, et en tel maniere c’on emplisse la ventouse d’aigue chaude soule, ou d’aigue ou il ait cuit herbes covenaubles a ceu.	The venous sores that are applied with no iron are those that are placed on the liver, and on the spleen, and gout, and on the belly and breast and loins and hip; for these members cannot endure being pierced above them, nor is this done except to draw blood from member to member, as we place them on the breasts when they bleed; to remove the viscosity that is in the member, as we put venous sores on the belly and breast to reduce the swelling; and to take away pain, when there is a boil or stone, it takes away the boil by its attractive virtue, or loosens the stone from its place; and thus it does when we put it on the liver and spleen when there is retained fluidity, and these venous sores are administered empty in order to suck only; and sometimes they are administered with fire, and sometimes filled with hot water mixed with a concoction of herbs suitable for those things.
Li forme de la ventouse aministré a feu si est ci:	The form of the ventouse administered with fire is this:
Et ait II dois de large et demi palme de parfont, et ait ou costeit entor lou mileu I petit pertus, ausi com por bouter une aguille, et soit d’arain, souef devant si qu’ele ne blece lou malaide au mettre sus lou membre; et ait em mileu une verge de travers d’arain ou de fer, por mettre une chandoile emprise, et en soient et grans et petites selonc les maladies et l’eaige de ces qui les prenent; car les ventouses des enfans sont autres que des vielz, et des maigres que des gras.	The incision should be two fingers and half a palm deep, and around the middle there should be a little pimple, as when one is to put in a needle, and it should be of lead, soft at the front so that it does not hurt the patient when putting on the member; and in the middle there should be a transverse bar of lead or iron, to put a candle fixed, and they should be great and small according to the illnesses and the age of those who take them; for the poultices of children are different from those of the old, and of thin people than of fat.
Et doit on ensi mettre ceste ventouse sus lou membre: empren la chandoile de cire ou I linement, et le met emmi la ventouse au traver de la verge qui est emmi la ventouse, si que li clartez soit au fons de la ventouse qu’elle ne brule le malaide; et puis la met sus lou membre, et tes dois soit sus lou pertus que nos avons dit, si que quant on vuet, si en va li vapor, et oste on la ventouse; et emprent on le linement si com devant, et le rencomence sovent, se tu as mestier.	And thus one should put this poultice on the member: take the candle of wax or linen, and put it inside the poultice at the transverse bar that is inside the poultice, so that the light be at the bottom of the poultice so as not to burn the patient; and then put it on the member, and it should be above the little pimple that we have said, so that when one wants, if there goes steam, and take off the poultice; and apply again the linen bandage just like before, and do this often, if you need to.
En la ventouse donques qui est aministree em maladie de pleuresin, a tout aigue, n’a point de verge ne de pertus, et n’est amenistree fuer que quant ele est plaine d’aigue, et est mise sus lou membre tant soulement; et c’est li forme:	In the bellows then which is administered in the disease of pleurisy, to all acute, there is no rod nor perforation, and it is not administered full except when it is full of water, and it is placed on the member alone.
Sansues ne sont amenistrees fuer que en membres ou on ne puet mettre ventouses por lour petitece, si com levres et gencives, et semblans leus; et en leus desnuez de char, si com doi ou neis, et autres semblans; et li qualitez de l’user si est que quant tu trueves sansues que sont en aigues douces nettes sens porreture, si les pren; et les met une nut en aigue douce, tant qu’elles soient famillouses, et n’aient rien en ventres; et puis soit sainiez de voinne et de ventouse, puis frote le membre tant k’il soit rouges, et met sus les sansues; et quant elles seront plainnes, et cherront, se c’est leu ou on puisse mettre ventouse, se li met, car ce vaut mout aprés.	Abscesses are not administered full except in members where one cannot put bellows because of their smallness, such as lips and gums, and similar places; and in bare bones without flesh, such as the elbow or knee, and others similar; and the quality of the abscess is that when you find abscesses that are in sweet clear water with a prick, take them; and put one in sweet clear water until they become soft and have nothing in their belly; and then lance the member with vein and bellows, then rub the member so much that it becomes red, and put on the abscesses; and when they are full and swollen, if it is a place where one can put a bellows, then put it, for this helps afterwards.
Se ceu non, si leve le leu d’aisil, et puis d’aigue, mout bien, et puis l’espreint; et se li leus saine trop aprés lou cors des sansues, si moille I drapel de lin en aigue froide, et met sus, tant que li sans soit estenchiés; et se li sans est multipliez, si met sus vitriole broié, ou gales, et semblans choses, tant qu’il soit estenchiez; ou tu met sor le leu moitiez de feves pelees, et les laisse tant qu’elles s’aerdent au leu, si estanchera maintenant; et se tu en as de plus mestier, si pren autres sansues.	If not, then raise the joint to a boil, and then in hot water, quite well, and then bind it; and if the wound becomes too wet with pus, then soak the cloth in cold water, and put on top, until the pus is closed; and if there is much pus, then apply vitriol boiled, or gall, and similar things, until it is closed; or you place half a handful of roasted broad beans on the joint, and let them stick to the joint, so that it will be closed now; and if you need more, take other poultices.
Et se les sansues ne se vuellent prendre, si frote le leu de sanc fres, ou tu i fiche une aguille tant que li sans en isse, et puis les met sus, et tantost com elles sentent lou sanc, si s’aerdent; et quant tu vues qu’elles chieent, si poudre sus un poc d’aloen et de seil broiet, ou de cendre, si cherront maintenant.	And if the poultices do not want to take hold, then rub the joint with fresh blood, or you put a needle into it so that the pus comes out, and then put them on top, and as soon as they feel the blood, they will stick; and when you see that they are discharging, then powder over a small amount of alum and burnt silk, or ash, so that they will stop now.
Ci comence li livre des brisures	The book of wounds begins here. (Note: this likely marks the end of the section on ventouses and the beginning of a new chapter in the medical text)
Cist chapistres est maintes fois necessaires en l’art de medicine, et est restorement de brisure et de desaluement.	This chapter is often necessary in the art of medicine, and is a treatment for wounds and swellings.
Saichiez, chier fil, que li fol mire se gietent de cest chapistre, qu’il ne virent onques nul des anciens livres, ne lettre n’en lurent; et por ceste cause est estraingie li science en nostre terre, car je n’en trovai onques nul boin; et n’en sai nient, fuer que par la science des anciens livres, ou j’ai tant estudiiet que je en ai traite la science, et par experiment, et par l’acostumance de ma vie; et je vos ai jai descrit en cest chapistre ceu qui est contenut, et l’ai abregié en la fin, et ai mostreit clerement les formes des estrumens que sont amenistrez, si com j’ai fait en ces II chapistres ci devant.	Be aware, dear son, that foolish doctors stray from this chapter, for they never read any of the ancient books, nor did they read about it; and because of this reason is the science in our land estranged, for I have not found any good ones; and I know nothing else, except by the knowledge of the ancient books which I have studied so much that I have treated the science and by experiment, and by the habit of my life; and I have described in this chapter what is contained, and I have shortened it at the end, and I have shown clearly the forms of the instruments that are brought, as I have done in these two chapters before.
Il covient donques que nos disons ou prologue de cest livre les sommes de la parole et les chapistres ou li saiges met son entendement, et demore sus la verteit, si que cil qui sont esproveit saichent et aprenent cest art noble, et l’aprenent a autrui.	It is therefore necessary that we say the prologue of this book, which contains the sum of words and chapters where the wise man sets his understanding, and remains on the truth, so that those who are proven know and learn this noble art, and teach it to others.
Je dis que quant brisure ou dislocation ou torsions ou quasseure vient a aucun, si covient que tu te hastes de sainier lou malaide d’une part et d’autre, s’aucune chose ne lou te tot, si com foiblece de cors, ou enfes, ou viellars; ou se li tens n’est tres chaus, ou trop frois; et menjuce cholz frois, c’est a savoir froides joutes, et char d’oiseil et de chevril, et ne boive point de vin, ne menjuce nules grasses chars, et ne s’emplisse mie trop de viandes qui remplissent les voinnes de sanc, tant que tu n’aies doute d’aposteme chaut, ne d’espandement de matiere au membre; si reviegne li malaides a som premerain governement k’il a useit.	I say that when fracture or dislocation or twisting or crushing comes to anyone, it is necessary for you to hasten to heal the patient from one side and the other, if nothing else prevents you, such as weakness of body, infancy, or old age; or if the time is not very hot or too cold; and cold morsels, that is to say cold juices of bird and of goat kid, and do not drink wine, nor eat any fat meats, and do not fill oneself too much with foods which fill the veins with blood, as long as you have no doubt of a hot abscess or of an effusion of matter into the member; if the patient returns to his first way of governing himself.
Et quant li os brisiés comence a restourer, si covient que on norrisse le malaide de viandes que font gros norrissement, ou il ait viscositeit, si com froment cuit en aigue, et ris, et testes, et piez, et pance de vaiche, et oefz, et poissons fres, et gros vin, et semblans chose.	And when the broken bone starts to heal, it is necessary that one nourishes the patient with meats that make thick nourishment, or have viscosity, such as boiled wheat, rice, cheese, and veal, and eggs, and fresh fish, and rich wine, and similar things.
Car a teil norrissement se reprent mues et plus tost li brisure.	For with proper nourishment the bone heals more quickly and sooner the breakage.
Et saichiez que li os brisiez des vielz et des decrepites ne se pueent mie bien reprendre ne souder selonc lor nature por la sechor des membres, ne cil ausi qui sont trop tendre, si com os de petis enfans; mais n’a cure, ramoinne sus l’os brisiet I gros tenrons qui s’aert et joint le membre ensamble, tant qu’il enforce si com il fut devant; et ne li tot point de son oevre, et por ceu le covient norrir de grosses viandes, si com nos avons dit.	And you should know that the broken bones of old people and those who are decrepit cannot be properly regained or soldered according to their nature due to the dryness of the limbs, nor also those that are too soft, such as the bones of small children; but do not worry, reset over the broken bone the large splints which have been tempered and join the member together, so that it strengthens itself just like it was before; and do not hold back from his work, and because of this he should be nourished with thick foods, such as we have said.
Et saiches que les briseures sont diverses em maintes manieres.	And know that breakages are diverse in many ways.
Car li brisure dou chief est autre que li brisure dou pis, ou de la jambe, et je vos dirai en cest chapistre toutes manieres de brisure.	For the breakage of the head is different from the breakage of the thigh or leg, and I will tell you in this chapter all the ways of breaking.
Car il i a brisure que vait a arvol, et autre en aguisant, si com li taille d’une penne por escrire.	For there are breaks that bend (or fracture) at a curve, and others in a crackling manner, such as the breakage of a quill pen for writing.
Et si a brisure avec plaie, et brisure a tout une petite fendure.	And also a break with an open wound, and a break with a small crack or fissure throughout its entire length.
Et a chescune maniere ait son engien, et son estrument propre en la restauration, selonc ceu que li remembrance vient en son leu; et est conue li brisure de l’os par ceu que li leus est tors, et apert bien par defuer, et sone au presseir de la main; et quant il n’i a tortuositeit ne son au tochier, et li malaides ne sent mie grant dolor, se n’i a point de brisure; mais il puet bien avoir tortuositeit, ou fendure, ou petite brisure.	And he should have his own device and proper instrument in the restoration, according to what the recollection comes into its place; and it is known that the break of the bone is by what the place is twisted, and appears well from outside, and sounds under the pressure of the hand; and when there is no twisting or noise at touch, and the patient does not feel great pain, then there is no break; but it can well have a twist, or crack, or small break.
Si ne covient mie que tu lou mueves a la main, mais met sus medicines que vienent ci aprés, et puis l’estraint maintenant subtilment.	So you should not move the hand at all, but put on medications that come next, and then bind it now gently.
Et saiches que li brisure que vait en arvol, et sont les II parties desjointes, il les covient maintenant mettre a point igalment, ainsois que apostemes chaus i viegne; et se apostemes chaulz i vient, si lou laisse aucun jor, tant que li apostemes soit apaisiez; puis le met igal au mues que tu pues legierement et saigement, et saiches que li restauration de li est plus legiere que li restaurations de celi ou il ait pieces; puis l’estraint, si com il covient ci aprés.	And know that the rupture which goes in a circle, and are the two parts disjointed, it is necessary now to put them in an equal point, so that suppurating swellings may come; and if suppurating swellings come, leave them alone for a day, until the swelling is appeased; then put him on even with the joint as you can easily and gently, and know that the restoration of this place is more easy than the restorations of those where there are pieces; then tighten it, as it must be afterwards.
Et se en l’os a piéces brisies, si l’estent de II pars, se c’est mains ou piet, ou a ta main se c’est uns petis membres, ou a II cordes, ou a une corde et a ta main; et met le membre selonc sa figure natureil, si que quant les parties dou membre brisiet sont desevrees, si ramoinne par toz engiens les additions en lor leus au mues que tu pues, et garde que par ton ovraige ne viegne au malaide lesion ne doulor; et ajoste bien les II chiés des os selonc les millors formes.	And if in the bone there are broken pieces, and it is held together by two parts, then it is hand or foot, or on your hand it is a small member, or on two strings, or one string and at your hand; and put the limb according to its natural figure, so that when the parts of the broken member are unseparated, you mend by all means the additions in their places as much as you can, and keep that through your work not a lesion nor pain may come to the patient; and join well the two joints of the bones according to the best forms.
Et le sent bien a la main s’il te semble k’il ne soit mie bien: si la dresce bellement, et ne l’estraindre mie trop, ne presser si com font cil qui i font venir par lor ovraige aposteme chaut et contraction au membre, si com nos avons veut maintes fois.	And if it seems to you that it is not well, feel it with your hand: straighten it beautifully and do not bind it too tightly or press it, as those who by their work bring about hot abscesses and contraction in the member, which we have warned against many times.
Et quant li membres est a point, si chastie bien lou malaide qu’il ne lou mueve, ne en veillant ne en dormant, ne en tornant, ne en autre maniere; et warde bien que li membres soit si asseurez qu’il ne sente nule dolor.	And when the limb is in place, then instruct the patient to stay still so that it does not move, neither watching nor sleeping, nor turning, nor in any other way; and take care that the limb be so secure that it feels no pain.
Car quant il sent nulle dolor, si n’est mie a point, et le covient mettre en teil disposition qu’il ne sent nule dolor; et te garde k’il soit mis droitement, que tortuositez n’i veigne quant il iert restorez; et li qualitez dou membre brisiet est selonc ceu que je te dis.	For when it feels no pain, then it is not in place, and you must put it in such a disposition that it feels no pain; and take care that it be set right, lest there come twisting in it when it has been restored; and the quality of the broken limb is according to what I am telling you.
Saiches que li membre brisiet sont divers em petitece et en grandor et en forme.	Know that broken members are diverse in smallness and greatness and in form.
Car il i a petis membres, si com brais et dois et semblans: si covient envoloupeir drapelz de lin molz et subtilz; et cil qui sont gros, si com cuixe et jambe: si covient envoloupeir d’un liien large et dur, car li larges liiens embraice tout le grant membre, et l’estraint de toutes pars igalment, si qu’il n’i a nule taiche; puis lie entor estoupes legieres, III tours ou IIII selonc lou membre, et estraint ta main un poc a tout le liien, puis vai au desus de la brisure et l’estraint moins, que la brisure puis esloigne un poc la brisure; et mollifie lou liien poc et poc, tant que tu preignes grant partie dou leu sain, puis repren I autre liien, et torne entor derechief sus lou leu brisiet; puis revai a la partie desoz de la brisure, et l’estraint moienement, ne mie trop fort ne trop molement, si com nos avons dit en la premiere lieure desus; puis met entre les liiens estoupes de lin ou drapelz, tant qu’il soit toz igalz, puis lie tout par desus I bendeil, et puis met tes esteles bien faites sus, s’il n’i a aposteme ou enfleure.	For there are small limbs, such as wrists and ankles and similar ones: then you must envelop them in soft and subtle linen cloth; and those that are large, such as thighs and legs: then you must envelop them with a wide and sturdy bandage, for the broad bands encircle the whole great limb and hold it straight from all sides, so that there is no slackness; then tie lightweight cloths around, three turns or four according to the limb, and hold your hand one finger's breadth away from the entire bandage, then go up above the break and bind it less tightly, so that the break will move one finger's breadth away; and soften the bandage little by little, until you take a great part of the healthy place, then take another bandage and wrap it around again on top of the broken limb; then go back to the lower part under the break and bind it moderately, not too tightly nor too loosely, just as we said in the first layer above; then put linen or cloth poultices between the bandages until they are entirely even, then tie everything with a headband above, and then put your well-made splints on top, unless there is an abscess or swelling.
Et s’il i a enfleure ou aposteme, si met sus chose apaisant aposteme chaut, et qui oste l’enfleure, et le laisse ensi aucuns jors; puis estrain sus tes esteles, et soient de moitiet d’une large cainne rese saigement, ou de fust dont on fait les clives, ou de rains de palme, et soient selonc ceste figure:	And if there is an abscess or swelling, then place a soothing hot poultice on the abscess and remove the inflammation; then bind it to the stars, with strips of cloth that are half as wide as a large dog's hide, or of wood for making skewers, or palm fronds, and in this shape:
mais il covient que li estele que om met sus la brisure soit un poc plus grosse et plus large que les autres; et soit ausi longe com il covient au membre de petitece ou de grandor; puis estraint sus les esteles ausi com tu as fait a la premiere lieure, puis lie desus un fil avenanment, si com en la premiere lieure sus la briseure; plus estroit est ensus, adés moins; et covient que li fil soient moien, ne trop gros ne trop menus, et soient de lin mol; car se li fil sont gros, si com j’ai veut des oevres des folz qui font lor lieures des cordes de linement tort, et c’est grant error, car li lieure ne puet estre igal s’il est trop subtilz, et ne covient mie qu’il ait entre chescune estele moins dou doi d’espaice.	but it is necessary that the star bandage placed on the break be a little thicker and wider than the others; and be as long as it is necessary for the smallness or largeness of the member; then bind the stars as you have done in the first treatment, then tie above with a thread appropriately, just as in the first treatment on the break; tighter up there, the less; and it is necessary that the thread be middle-sized, neither too thick nor too thin, and be made of soft linen; for if the threads are thick, like I've seen the works of fools who make their bandages with twisted linen strings, and that's a great error, because the bandage can't be equal if it is too fine, and it does not need to have between each star less than two fingers' width.
Et se li malaides est navrez par les chiés des esteles aprés l’estrignement en leus sains, si met desoz elles estoupes de lin, ou lainne charpie, si qu’il ne se blece.	And if the patient is cut by the splints after they are tightened in their proper place, then put underneath them linen cloths or lint to prevent bleeding.
Quant li plaie est avec la briseure, et li keurs est rompus, je en dirai la cure ci aprés.	When the wound is with the break and the ligament is broken, I will say how it should be cured later on.
Et saiches qu’il ne covient mie estraindre d’esteles toz les membres brisiez le premier jor.	And know that you must not bind with splints all the broken members at first.
Car quant li membres est grans, se n’i covient mie mettre esteles fuer que aprés V jors ou VII, ou plus selonc ta seurteit d’aposteme chaut sorvenant; et vez ci la maniere des emplaustres anciiens por restorer brisure, dislocation, et torsion: et est que tu pren de la farine que se tient as parois dou molin, et la confis avec abun d’uef, et met ceste confection, ne trop clere ne trop espesse, et en use, et c’est li commune maniere de l’emplaustre restorant la brisure a plusors dispositions, et meismement as femmes et as petis enfans, car il n’est mie ne trop chas ne trop frois.	When the limbs are large, there is no need to apply poultices too soon, that is after 5 days or 7, or more according to your discretion of hot abscess: and here is the manner of ancient ointments for restoring breakage, dislocation, and torsion: and it is that you take flour that remains on the walls of the mill, and mix it with a lot of egg white, and put this concoction not too clear nor too thick, and use it, and this is the common manner of the ointment restoring the breakage to many dispositions, and indeed to women and small children, because it is neither too hot nor too cold.
Autre maniere d’emplastre restorans la brisure et la dislocation et la torsion: pren З abareis et ladani et enule et asefetide et gomme arabique et mugat, ana .x. З.; mirre et aloes, ana .v. Another manner of poultice restoring breaks, dislocations, and torsions: take 3 parts of aloes, gum arabic, myrrh, and bezoar stone, and mix with 10 parts of resin; add 5 parts of mirre and aloe, and 8 parts of alachil; reduce to powder, and mix with plenty of egg. Another manner of ointment that is good for torsion: take myrrh and vernaline, each 10 drachmas; balsam and aloes, each 9 drachmas; aconite 6 drachmas; Armenian balm 10 drachmas, and grind well, and mix with water or a lot of egg white, and administer.

Another manner of plastering joints and bones which have been dislocated from their places, and soothe the pain occurring; and they use the breakage: take new linen, and moisten it in ashes and cooked oil, and put on top of the joint.
Cist emplastres ne restoure mie, mais il est boins por apaisier d’aposteme chaut, et oste proprement les dolors.	These plasters do not restore [it], but they are good for soothing hot abscesses and properly removing the pains.
Autre maniere d’emplaustre qui restoure les os brisiez: pren foilles de figuier seches, et foilles de pavol terrestre, et broie ensamble, et fai emplaustre.	Another manner of poultice that restores broken bones: take dry leaves of fig tree and dry leaves of wild pomegranate, and pound them together to make a poultice.
Autre maniere d’emplaustre a la restoration de la brisure de l’os et por amenusier lou remenant de l’aposteme.	Another manner of plastering for the restoration of the breakage of the bone and to reduce the remaining part of the abscess.

Take roots of marshmallow, and seeds of flax, balm, milk thistle, and fennel, and flowers of violets and chamomile, all in equal parts; grind, and mix with salt water, or sweet water, or with wine, according to the heat of the member, and its rest.
Ypocras dist en son livre c’on ne mette sus lou membre brisiet por restorer fuer que cerote fait de cire et d’oile, et nulle autre chose plus, et dist qu’il soit entre dous ne trop molz ne trop durs.	Hippocrates says in his book that when restoring a broken member, one should only apply ointment made of wax and oil on it, and nothing else, and that it should be between two extremes, neither too soft nor too hard.
Et il semble galien c’on doit mettre sus lou membre brisiet, por restoreir, choses seches un poc chaudes, si com mirre et aloes et encens et semblans choses, si com nos avons dit.	And Galen seems to say that when restoring a broken member, one should apply dry and slightly warm things on it, such as myrrh and aloe and incense and similar things, which we have already mentioned.
Autre maniere d’emplaustre que vaut a lesion et a dolor: pren mugath et chevolz d’omes decopez, ou pennes d’oiseil, et vismalve, et seil igalment, et les broie, et en fai emplaustre et lou lie.	Another manner of poultice that is good for lesions and pain: take myrrh and the hairs from a depilated man or goose feathers, and equal parts of vernalis, and pound them together to make a poultice.
Et puis lou deslie un poc aprés, et pren garde se li malaides ne sent nule dolor, ne ne s’est meus li os de son leu; si ne lou deslie par mains jors Et se dolor vient au leu, ou prurite, sens repos ou enfleure, si te haste, et le deslie, et oste l’emplaustre maintenant; et pren drapeil legier, et esponge de mer moiste, et plonge en aigue, et en leve lou leu, tant que li prurite cesse, et soit apaisie li dolor; puis laisse reposer lou membre une houre, puis met sus lainne seche, moillie en aigue ou en oile roset, et lie sus une nut, tant que tu soies seur d’aposteme chaut; et repose li enflammation dou membre, et soit ostee li dolor, puis rencomence la lieure subtil, et un poc d’emplaustre, et ne l’estraindre mie si fort com devant; et subtilie tant qu’il soit sanez.	And then loosen the poultice after a while, and take care that the patient feels no pain, nor has the bone moved from its place; if it does not loosen by hand in two days.
